import torch
import torch.nn as nn

# Default ModelNew is PyTorch-only. run_and_check: use check_kernel=False with backend=cuda
# until the candidate file contains a real CUDA kernel (see kernel_static_checker).

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Fused 1D convolution + GroupNorm for temporal sequences (N, C, T).

    Typical in time-series heads, audio / motor trajectories, and sequence models
    that use strided or padded Conv1d followed by per-channel normalization without
    collapsing the time dimension (GroupNorm over `out_channels` on (N, C_out, T')).

    Reference fusion target: `Conv1d` → `GroupNorm` (no activation), matching a
    common stem or residual branch in temporal backbones.
    """

    def __init__(
        self,
        in_channels: int,
        out_channels: int,
        num_groups: int,
        kernel_size: int = 3,
        stride: int = 1,
        padding: int = 1,
        dilation: int = 1,
        eps: float = 1e-5,
        affine: bool = True,
    ):
        super().__init__()
        assert out_channels % num_groups == 0
        self.conv = nn.Conv1d(
            in_channels,
            out_channels,
            kernel_size,
            stride=stride,
            padding=padding,
            dilation=dilation,
            bias=False,
        )
        self.norm = nn.GroupNorm(
            num_groups=num_groups,
            num_channels=out_channels,
            eps=eps,
            affine=affine,
        )

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            x: (N, C_in, T) temporal feature map
        Returns:
            (N, C_out, T') with T' determined by conv stride / padding / kernel
        """
        return self.norm(self.conv(x))


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    """
    KernelBench candidate entry point. Replace with a fused CUDA/Triton kernel;
    default delegates to `Model` for harness smoke tests.
    """

    def __init__(
        self,
        in_channels: int,
        out_channels: int,
        num_groups: int,
        kernel_size: int = 3,
        stride: int = 1,
        padding: int = 1,
        dilation: int = 1,
        eps: float = 1e-5,
        affine: bool = True,
    ):
        super().__init__()
        self._impl = Model(
            in_channels,
            out_channels,
            num_groups,
            kernel_size=kernel_size,
            stride=stride,
            padding=padding,
            dilation=dilation,
            eps=eps,
            affine=affine,
        )

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return self._impl(x)


batch_size = 16
in_channels = 128
out_channels = 256
num_groups = 32
seq_len = 1024
kernel_size = 3
stride = 1
padding = 1


def get_inputs():
    x = torch.randn(batch_size, in_channels, seq_len)
    return [x]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [
        in_channels,
        out_channels,
        num_groups,
        kernel_size,
        stride,
        padding,
    ]

The output of conv is not just the GEMM; we need to apply GroupNorm. GroupNorm operates per group over channels and time. Since our block computes a tile of channels and time, we cannot compute mean/var within a single block unless the block covers the entire group and entire time. Our GEMM block covers only 32 channels and 64 time steps. A group has 8 channels and 1024 time steps. So our block covers multiple groups (32/8=4 groups) and a subset of time. Therefore computing group norm within this block is not straightforward because we need cross-block reductions. Then we can compute partial sums per block and reduce across blocks that share the same (n, group). This is similar to our earlier idea. Let's reconsider the single-kernel block-per-group approach. We can make it efficient enough for this problem size? Let's analyze the naive conv approach with shared weights. Block per (n, g). Block processes all channels in group (cpg = C_out / num_groups) and all time steps (T_out). Let's say blockDim.x = 256 threads. Each thread handles a subset of T_out and all channels in the group. We load weights for the group into shared memory: size = cpg * C_in * K floats. We also need to compute conv. Let's have each thread iterate over t from tid to T_out-1 step blockDim.x. For each t, each thread computes conv for all co in group: ``` float out[CPG_MAX]; // if cpg is small, say up to 32, we can use a fixed-size array or dynamic. ``` But `cpg` might vary. Since `out_channels % num_groups == 0`, `cpg` is integer. Could be large. If cpg is large (e.g., 256), storing per-thread outputs for all co is too many registers. Instead, we can loop over co: ``` for (int co = 0; co < cpg; ++co) { float sum = 0; for (int ci = 0; ci < in_channels; ++ci) { for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; ++k) { int in_t = t * stride - padding + k * dilation; float xval = (in_t >= 0 && in_t < in_len) ? input[...] : 0; float wval = sweight[(co * in_channels + ci) * kernel_size + k]; sum += xval * wval; } } // accumulate sum and sum_sq local_sum += sum; local_sum_sq += sum * sum; // maybe write sum to some local storage? No, we recompute later. } ``` This per-thread per-t does `cpg * in_channels * kernel_size` MACs. If cpg=8, in_channels=128, K=3 => 3072 MACs per t. If blockDim.x=256, total MACs per block per wave = 256*3072 = 786,432. For T_out=1024, each thread handles ~4 time steps, total block MACs = 1024*3072 = 3,145,728. That's not huge. FLOPs per block = ~6.3M. For 512 blocks, total ~3.2B FMAs (as before). At 20 TFLOPS, 160 us per pass. Two passes = 320 us. This might be acceptable if memory is also limited. But will a naive loop be efficient? The inner loops are over 128 and 3. If we unroll the K loop (since K is small), the compiler can optimize. The load of `xval` from global memory is coalesced across threads if threads process consecutive t. Let's check: each thread accesses `input[n, ci, t*stride - padding + k*dilation]`. For fixed ci and k, consecutive threads have consecutive t, so accesses are coalesced along the time dimension (the last dimension). Good. However we have loops over ci and k, so each thread does many loads. Since 256 threads load 256 consecutive time positions for a given ci and k, these loads are coalesced into 256 contiguous floats (1024 bytes) per warp. That's good. Weights are loaded from shared memory. The inner loop over ci loads weight for each ci, k. Each thread loads the same weight? No, for a given co, ci, k, all threads for different t load the same weight value (since weight doesn't depend on t). Shared memory broadcasts this to all threads. That's efficient. So the naive conv loop actually has very good memory access patterns! The main cost is the loop overhead and instruction count. But modern GPUs have high throughput for FMAs. Let's compute the arithmetic intensity: Each MAC requires 2 floats read (x and w) and 1 FMA. x is read from global memory (L2 cached), w from shared memory. Global memory bandwidth is high but if data is cached (since each x element is reused across co and k), it might stay in L1/L2. For a given t and ci, x is loaded once and used for all co and k? Wait, for a given t, ci, and k, the x value is `x[n, ci, t*stride - padding + k*dilation]`. For different co, the x value is the same (since conv is over channels). So if we loop over co as outer and ci,k inner, x is loaded many times. Better to loop over ci and k outer, and co inner, so x is loaded once and multiplied with all co's weights. Restructure: ``` for each t: float local_sum[cpg] = {0}; // if cpg small for ci in 0..C_in-1: for k in 0..K-1: float xval = load_input(...); for co in 0..cpg-1: float w = sweight[(co*C_in + ci)*K + k]; local_sum[co] += xval * w; ``` Now x is loaded C_in*K times per t, reused across cpg. For cpg=8, this reduces global loads by factor 8 compared to co-outer loop. Good. But `local_sum` needs cpg registers. For cpg up to maybe 32, that's okay. For cpg=8, it's 8 registers per thread. Then after the loops, each thread has `local_sum[co]` for its t. It can accumulate global sums and sum_sq. Then after reduction, it can do a second pass (or reuse the values) to normalize and write. Let me think about designing the custom GroupNorm kernel. Input: `y` shape (N, C_out, T_out). We need to group channels: num_groups groups, each with cpg channels. For each n and g, compute mean and variance over cpg * T_out elements. Then normalize each element. Approach A: one block per (n, g). Block loads group into shared memory, computes reduction, normalizes, writes back. Requirements: `cpg * T_out * sizeof(float) <= max_shared_mem` (e.g., 48KB). For default, it's 32KB. Good. But block size should be enough to cover all elements for parallel loading and reduction. We can use 256 or 512 threads. Let's parameterize: ``` const int GROUP_THREADS = 256; ``` Each thread loads multiple elements. Shared memory size = `cpg * T_out` floats. Algorithm: ``` __global__ void group_norm_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int T, int num_groups, float eps, bool affine) { int n = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int cpg = C / num_groups; int c0 = g * cpg; int tid = threadIdx.x; int nt = blockDim.x; int group_size = cpg * T; extern __shared__ float sdata[]; float* s = sdata; // size = group_size // Load group data into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < group_size; idx += nt) { int co = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c = c0 + co; float val = input[((n * C + c) * T + t)]; s[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Reduction for mean float sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < group_size; idx += nt) { sum += s[idx]; } // block reduce sum __shared__ float s_sum[GROUP_THREADS]; s_sum[tid] = sum; __syncthreads(); for (int offset = nt/2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (tid < offset) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / group_size; // Reduction for var float sq_diff = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < group_size; idx += nt) { float diff = s[idx] - mean; sq_diff += diff * diff; } s_sum[tid] = sq_diff; __syncthreads(); for (int offset = nt/2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (tid < offset) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_sum[0] / group_size; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); __syncthreads(); // Normalize and write back for (int idx = tid; idx < group_size; idx += nt) { int co = idx / T; int t = idx % T; float val = s[idx]; float norm = (val - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { int c = c0 + co; norm = norm * gamma[c] + beta[c]; } int c = c0 + co; output[((n * C + c) * T + t)] = norm; } } ``` This kernel does: 1. Read input into shared memory (1 global read). 2. Compute mean and var (reads from shared). 3. Normalize and write output (1 global write). No extra reads. So 1 read + 1 write total. Compared to PyTorch's GroupNorm: likely more than 1 read + 1 write. So this saves bandwidth. Also it fuses the affine transform. And it uses shared memory which is fast. But we also need to handle cases where group_size > max_shared_mem. We can add a check: if `cpg * T * sizeof(float) > 48*1024`, fallback to PyTorch. We can also vectorize loads/stores. For T contiguous, we can load `float4`. Since group elements are stored as (C, T) with T contiguous, for a fixed co, t is contiguous. So for a thread loading a chunk of `idx`, if we load 4 consecutive idx (which correspond to t, t+1, t+2, t+3 for fixed co), they are contiguous in global memory. We can use `float4` loads. Implementation with vectorization: ``` int group_size = cpg * T; int idx = tid * 4; for (; idx + 3 < group_size; idx += nt * 4) { int co = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c = c0 + co; // load float4 from input float4 val4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(input + ((n * C + c) * T + t))[0]; s[idx] = val4.x; s[idx+1] = val4.y; s[idx+2] = val4.z; s[idx+3] = val4.w; } for (; idx < group_size; idx += nt) { ... } ``` But writing into shared memory in a strided pattern might cause bank conflicts if not careful. However `s[idx]` where idx is consecutive is fine (consecutive threads write to consecutive shared memory). The `float4` load from global is coalesced. Similarly for reduction and write-back. But we need to be careful about alignment: `((n * C + c) * T + t)` must be 16-byte aligned for `float4`. For t=0, if `T` is not a multiple of 4, then the address might not be aligned. Let's check: address offset = `n*C*T + c*T + t`. For t=0, offset = `T*c` mod 16. Since `T` could be 1024, `T*4 = 4096` bytes, which is divisible by 16. So for any c multiple of 1, `c*T*4` is divisible by 16 if `T*4 % 16 == 0` => `T % 4 == 0`. For T=1024, yes. But for generic T not divisible by 4, alignment is not guaranteed. We can still use `float4` only when the base address is aligned and within bounds. We can compute alignment: `base_ptr = input + ((n * C + c0) * T);`. For each co, `ptr_co = base_ptr + co * T`. Since `T*4` must be multiple of 16 for all co. So if `T % 4 == 0`, alignment holds. Otherwise, we fall back to scalar loads. For reduction, we can also vectorize: sum four floats at a time. This is a bit tricky with the mean reduction. But we can accumulate a float4 sum locally. Let's think about performance. The kernel is memory-bound: it reads group_size floats and writes group_size floats. group_size = 8192 floats = 32KB per (n,g). For 512 groups, total reads = 16MB, writes = 16MB. At 900GB/s, that's ~36us. Plus reduction overhead (negligible). So kernel time maybe ~40us. Conv might be ~30us. Total ~70us. Reference might be conv 30us + PyTorch GN maybe 30us = 60us. Then our kernel might be slower because it loads entire group into shared memory (which takes time) and PyTorch GN might be more optimized (using warp shuffles and not loading into shared memory). Wait, loading into shared memory is just a copy; it's not necessarily slower than loading into registers if we need to read multiple times. But PyTorch's GN might be cache-friendly and avoid explicit shared memory. Our kernel's shared memory might cause occupancy issues if we use too much. If `group_size = 32KB` shared memory per block, then with 48KB max per SM, we can have at most 1 block per SM. Occupancy is limited. But we have 512 blocks (16*32). If the GPU has 80 SMs, then 512 blocks / 80 = 6.4 waves. With 1 block per SM, each SM processes 6 or 7 blocks serially. If each block takes ~10us (just a guess), total time ~70us. Not bad. But if shared memory is 32KB, we might be able to use only 1 block per SM. On V100, max shared is 96KB if configured. Actually shared memory limit is 96KB on some arch if we call `cudaFuncSetAttribute`. But by default, 48KB. Can we set it to 96KB? On compute capability >= 7.x, we can set `cudaFuncSetAttribute` with `cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize` up to 96KB or 99KB. That might allow more. But for 32KB, 48KB is fine. Wait, but we can also reduce shared memory by not loading the entire group. Instead, we can compute mean and var using a parallel reduction algorithm without storing all data in shared memory, using just a few shared floats for reduction. We can load data directly from global memory for reduction, and then load again for normalization. That is 2 reads + 1 write. But we avoid shared memory limit. However, the first approach (1 read + 1 write) is better if we have enough shared memory. Occupancy with 32KB shared memory and 256 threads: On A100, each SM has 164KB shared memory. So 32KB allows 5 blocks per SM if register file permits. On V100, 96KB shared, so 3 blocks per SM. So occupancy is okay. Let's check GPU specs. Let me design the kernel more concretely: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv1d_groupnorm_kernel( const float* input, const float* weight, const float* gamma, const float* beta, float* output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int T_in, int T_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int num_groups, float eps, bool affine ) { // Each block: one (n, group) pair int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; if (n >= N || g >= num_groups) return; int c_per_group = C_out / num_groups; int group_start = g * c_per_group; // Shared memory: store conv outputs for this group // Size: c_per_group * T_out floats extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; // Each thread handles some (c, t) outputs int total_outputs = c_per_group * T_out; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; // Compute conv outputs for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += num_threads) { int c_local = idx / T_out; int t = idx % T_out; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) { int t_in = t * stride - padding + k * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (t_in >= 0 && t_in < T_in) { val = input[((n * C_in) + c_in) * T_in + t_in]; } sum += val * weight[((c_out * C_in) + c_in) * K + k]; } } s_mem[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute mean float mean = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += num_threads) { mean += s_mem[idx]; } // Warp reduction for mean // ... // Block reduction for mean // ... // Similar for variance // Normalize and write output // ... } ``` Wait, this has a problem: block reduction with `__syncthreads()` where not all threads participate in the loop can deadlock if the loop iterations differ. In CUDA, if we use `for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += num_threads)` with `__syncthreads()` inside, all threads in the block must execute the `__syncthreads()` the same number of times. Since the loop divides evenly only if total_outputs is a multiple of num_threads, we need to be careful. Actually, if total_outputs / num_threads can have different ceiling for different warps? No, all threads in a block use the same num_threads and the loop condition is the same, so they all execute the same number of iterations IF we write it correctly. But if some threads in a warp diverge... actually no, there's no divergence in the loop condition `idx < total_outputs` - all threads in a warp evaluate it the same because tid differs by multiples within the warp, and all threads in the block follow the same control flow pattern. No race because each thread writes unique indices. Then one `__syncthreads()` after the loop. For the reduction, we can also do it without internal sync if we use sequential addressing properly. Let me design a cleaner kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_conv1d_groupnorm_kernel(...) { // Shared memory layout: [conv_outputs][mean_storage][var_storage] // But we can compute mean and var without extra shared memory using a reduction tree. // Step 1: Compute conv outputs for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += blockDim.x) { // compute conv... s_mem[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: Compute mean using parallel reduction // We can do a thread-local partial sum, then warps shuffle, then block reduction float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += blockDim.x) { local_sum += s_mem[idx]; } // Warp shuffle reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local_sum, offset); } // Store warp sums to shared memory __shared__ float warp_sums[32]; // max 32 warps per block int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; if (lane == 0) warp_sums[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Final reduction by warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float block_sum = (tid < (blockDim.x + 31) / 32) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, block_sum, offset); } if (tid == 0) { s_mem[total_outputs] = block_sum / total_outputs; // store mean } } __syncthreads(); // Step 3: Compute variance float mean = s_mem[total_outputs]; float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += blockDim.x) { float diff = s_mem[idx] - mean; local_sq += diff * diff; } // same warp shuffle... float variance; if (tid == 0) { variance = warp_sum / total_outputs + eps; // wait, need proper reduction s_mem[total_outputs + 1] = variance; } __syncthreads(); // Step 4: Normalize and write float var = s_mem[total_outputs + 1]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var); for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T_out; int t = idx % T_out; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = (s_mem[idx] - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { val = val * gamma[c_out] + beta[c_out]; } output[((n * C_out) + c_out) * T_out + t] = val; } } ``` Hmm, but for the variance reduction I also need the same pattern. However, for small C_in, C_out, and K, direct conv can be competitive or faster than cuDNN because of cuDNN's overhead. Let me do some back-of-the-envelope. For our config: - 1.6B MACs - cuDNN might achieve 50-80% of peak for this size. At 67 TFLOPS, that's 33-53 TFLOPS effective, so ~30-60 microseconds. - Our direct conv: 3.2 GFLOPs at maybe 30-50% of peak (due to less optimized access patterns) = 10-20 TFLOPS effective, so ~160-320 microseconds. Hmm, that would be much slower than cuDNN! And if GroupNorm takes only ~10-20 microseconds, then being 5x slower on conv would be bad. But wait, with fusion, we avoid the 16.8MB intermediate write+read, saving ~10 microseconds. So if our conv takes 200 microseconds and groupnorm takes 20, total 220. PyTorch: conv 50us + groupnorm 20us = 70us. We'd be 3x slower. That's not good. We need our direct conv to be reasonably fast. To make direct conv faster, some techniques: 1. Use shared memory tiles for input 2. Unroll the K loop 3. Use vectorized loads 4. Process multiple output channels per thread 5. Tile the computation more carefully But actually, for C_in=128, K=3, that's only 384 MACs per output. Even with perfect efficiency, at 67 TFLOPS, 512 blocks * 8192 outputs * 384 MACs * 2 = 3.2 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, this is 48 microseconds. If we achieve 50% efficiency, it's 100 microseconds. That's actually comparable to cuDNN for small sizes! Let me think about achievable efficiency. Each thread does 32 outputs * 384 MACs = 12,288 MACs = 24,576 FLOPs. With 256 threads per block, 512 blocks, total threads = 131K. Each thread does 24K FLOPs. For 100% utilization, we'd need instruction-level parallelism and good memory access. The inner loop over c_in (128) and k (3) does 384 iterations. Each iteration has: 2 loads (input, weight), 1 FMA, some address arithmetic. So the loop is memory-instruction heavy but the FMAs can hide latency. Actually, if weights are in shared memory, each load is one shared memory instruction. On H100, shared memory has high bandwidth and low latency. Input is global memory, but with coalesced access. The compute-to-memory ratio: 384 FMAs per output. Each FMA = 2 FLOPs. Memory: input loads = 384 floats (but with overlap, actually less due to stride=1, padding=1, and adjacent threads sharing input). Wait, thread tid loads input at t, t-1, t+1 for each c_in. Thread tid+1 loads input at t+1, t, t+2. So for adjacent threads, 2 out of 3 input values overlap. With 32 threads in a warp, they collectively load input for times t-1 to t+32, which is 34 values per c_in. Instead of 32*3=96 loads, they can be satisfied with 34 loads if well-cached. L1 cache on H100 is very good. So effective memory traffic is more like 34 input + 1 weight broadcast per c_in. That's 35 loads for 384 FMAs. So compute-to-memory ratio is good. I think we can achieve reasonable efficiency, maybe 30-50%. That would make the conv competitive with cuDNN for this small size. Let me also think about another optimization: use `__restrict__` and compile with `-O3` and `-use_fast_math`. Another idea: tile the input in shared memory. For a time chunk of, say, 64 positions, load input for all C_in channels and that time chunk. Then compute conv for all outputs in that chunk. This reduces global memory loads by reusing input values across output positions. For time chunk size 64, input needed is 64+2 = 66 time positions (with padding=1) for 128 channels = 8,448 floats = 33.8 KB. Plus output storage for 8 channels * 64 time = 512 floats = 2 KB. Total 36 KB shared memory. Plus weights 12 KB = 48 KB. That's reasonable. Processing: each block handles one (n, group) and multiple time tiles serially. For each tile: 1. Load input for that tile to shared memory 2. Compute conv outputs using shared input and shared weights 3. Store conv output in shared memory 4. Accumulate local statistics After all tiles, compute final mean/variance, then traverse tiles again to normalize and write to global output. This approach reduces global memory traffic significantly: instead of each thread loading input 384 times per output, we load each input element once per tile and reuse it. Let me detail this more: For a time tile of T_tile = 64: - Input shared memory: C_in * (T_tile + K - 1) floats = 128 * 66 = 8448 floats = 33.8 KB - Output shared memory for this tile: c_per_group * T_tile = 8 * 64 = 512 floats = 2 KB - Weights: c_per_group * C_in * K = 8 * 128 * 3 = 3072 floats = 12.3 KB - Total per tile: ~48 KB For T_out=1024, we need 1024/64 = 16 tiles. For each tile, we: 1. Load input tile to shared memory (all 256 threads, 8448/256 ≈ 33 floats each) 2. Compute outputs for the tile (8*64=512 outputs, 512/256=2 outputs per thread) 3. Store in shared output buffer and accumulate local sum/sum_sq Between tiles, we accumulate mean and variance variables in registers. After all 16 tiles, we have the full mean and variance for the group. Then we traverse tiles again, loading input, computing conv, normalizing, and writing to global memory. Wait, we could keep the conv outputs in shared memory... but 16 tiles * 512 outputs = 8192 outputs = 32 KB total. We could just process all tiles for conv first, keeping outputs in shared memory. But 32KB for outputs + 33.8KB for input tile + 12.3KB for weights = 78KB, which fits! Actually, if we keep ALL outputs in shared memory, we don't need the tiling approach at all! We already established that 8192 outputs = 32KB fits. So the tiling approach doesn't help for this config. But for larger configs where outputs don't fit, tiling would be necessary. For our config, the simplest approach works fine. Let me just implement it. Actually, I want to think about one more thing: whether to load weights to shared memory or not. Shared memory for weights: 12.3 KB. This is small. The benefit is that each thread reads weights from fast shared memory instead of global memory. The downside is slightly larger shared memory usage. Without weight shared memory: 32KB outputs + small reduction space = ~33KB. With weight SM: 45KB. Both are well under limits. Let me add weight caching to shared memory. It should help. But wait, for large configs, adding weights to SM might exceed the limit. So I'll add a runtime check: if (c_per_group * C_in * K + c_per_group * T_out) * 4 < 48 * 1024 (or some fraction of max), load weights to SM. Otherwise load from global. Actually, for simplicity, I can just always load weights to SM and hope it fits for the test config. But to be robust and avoid silent failures, I should check. Actually, if shared memory exceeds the limit, CUDA will refuse to launch the kernel and return an error. One more consideration: I should use `__inline__` or `__forceinline__` for helper functions. Actually, I'll just write everything in one kernel. Let me write the CUDA kernel: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> template <typename T> __device__ __forceinline__ T warp_reduce_sum(T val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void fused_conv1d_groupnorm_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int T_in, int T_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation, int num_groups, float eps, bool affine ) { int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; if (n >= N || g >= num_groups) return; int c_per_group = C_out / num_groups; int group_start = g * c_per_group; int total_outputs = c_per_group * T_out; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_output = s_mem; float* s_weights = s_output + total_outputs; int tid = threadIdx.x; int block_size = blockDim.x; // Load weights for this group into shared memory int weight_size = c_per_group * C_in * K; for (int idx = tid; idx < weight_size; idx += block_size) { s_weights[idx] = weight[group_start * C_in * K + idx]; } // Compute conv outputs for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += block_size) { int c_local = idx / T_out; int t = idx % T_out; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float sum = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; ++c_in) { int t_base = t * stride - padding; int w_base = ((c_local * C_in) + c_in) * K; for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int t_in = t_base + k * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (t_in >= 0 && t_in < T_in) { val = input[((n * C_in) + c_in) * T_in + t_in]; } sum += val * s_weights[w_base + k]; } } s_output[idx] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // Compute mean using two-level reduction float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += block_size) { local_sum += s_output[idx]; } local_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); __shared__ float warp_mean_sums[32]; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; if (lane == 0) warp_mean_sums[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float block_sum = (lane < (block_size + 31) >> 5) ? warp_mean_sums[lane] : 0.0f; block_sum = warp_reduce_sum(block_sum); if (lane == 0) { s_output[total_outputs] = block_sum / (float)total_outputs; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_output[total_outputs]; // Compute variance float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += block_size) { float diff = s_output[idx] - mean; local_sq += diff * diff; } local_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq); if (lane == 0) warp_mean_sums[warp_id] = local_sq; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float block_sq = (lane < (block_size + 31) >> 5) ? warp_mean_sums[lane] : 0.0f; block_sq = warp_reduce_sum(block_sq); if (lane == 0) { s_output[total_outputs + 1] = block_sq / (float)total_outputs; } } __syncthreads(); float variance = s_output[total_outputs + 1] + eps; float inv_std = rsqrtf(variance); // Normalize and write output for (int idx = tid; idx < total_outputs; idx += block_size) { int c_local = idx / T_out; int t = idx % T_out; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = (s_output[idx] - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { val = val * gamma[c_out] + beta[c_out]; } output[((n * C_out) + c_out) * T_out + t] = val; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle bug. In the variance section, I'm using `warp_mean_sums` which I wrote to in the mean section. But after `__syncthreads()`, warp 0 should be able to read it. Actually, I think it's fine because all threads in the block reach the `__syncthreads()` at the end of the mean section before any thread proceeds to the variance section. But wait, warp 0 might finish the mean computation and immediately proceed to variance while other warps haven't finished storing their mean sums? No, because there's a `__syncthreads()` after the warp mean sums are written by all warps, and warp 0 is gated by `if (warp_id == 0)`. The `__syncthreads()` ensures all warps have written before warp 0 reads. Actually, hold on. reasoning
The CUDA kernel needs to be in a .cu file with proper CUDA syntax. The issue is that `dim3` and the kernel launch syntax need to be inside the CUDA source, not the C++ source. But `load_inline` takes both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. The C++ wrapper in `cpp_sources` can't contain CUDA-specific syntax like `dim3` or `<<< >>>`. I need to put the wrapper function in the `cuda_sources` instead. Or I can use a function call from the C++ side and define the host-side function in the CUDA source. Actually, looking at `load_inline`, the `functions` parameter lists functions from the `cpp_sources`. The C++ source needs to call the kernel somehow. But wait - I can define a regular C++ function in the `cuda_sources` that does the kernel launch, and then declare it in the `cpp_sources`. Actually, the more common pattern is: 1. Define the kernel in `cuda_sources` 2. Define a host wrapper function (that calls the kernel) in `cuda_sources` as well, using `extern "C"` or just global function 3. The `cpp_sources` just needs the declaration of this host wrapper Wait, but `load_inline` builds them together, so I think I can just put everything in `cuda_sources` and use an empty `cpp_sources`. Or I can put both in `cuda_sources`. Actually, a simpler approach: put all the code in `cuda_sources` and have no `cpp_sources`. Let me check if that works. Actually, looking at PyTorch docs: `load_inline(cpp_sources, cuda_sources, functions, ...)`. I think I can just put the host function in the cuda_sources. But `functions` refers to functions exposed to Python from the cpp_sources... Actually, pybind11 auto-generates bindings. The `functions` array lists function names that must be available. Let me try a simpler approach - put everything in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty or with declarations. Actually, the simplest is to define the host function in `cuda_sources` as well, and just forward declare it in `cpp_sources`. But honestly, when using `load_inline`, you can also use a single `cuda_sources` that includes both kernel and host wrapper. The auto-generated pybind11 module will link against the function. Let me try putting everything in `cuda_sources`, and just have a declaration in `cpp_sources`. Or just put both kernel + wrapper in `cuda_sources`, with an empty or minimal `cpp_sources`. Actually I think the pattern should be: - `cpp_sources`: just forward declarations of the functions listed in `functions` - `cuda_sources`: kernel + host wrapper that calls the kernel Wait, but the generated pybind11 bindings need the function definition to be available at link time. Since both .cpp and .cu files are compiled and linked together, a declaration in cpp file and definition in cu file should work. Let me try with cpp_sources containing only the function declaration (or a `extern` declaration) and cuda_sources containing the actual kernel + wrapper.

reasoning
The issue is that the function declaration needs to be in the C++ source when using `cpp_sources`, or when putting everything in `cuda_sources`, it seems the pybind wrapper is generated in the main.cpp but can't find the CUDA function because it's only declared in the .cu file. Actually the issue says `fused_conv1d_groupnorm` was not declared in this scope in main.cpp. This happens because `load_inline` generates the pybind11 code in main.cpp which includes the function declaration via `cpp_sources`. If we put everything in `cuda_sources`, the function declaration is not visible in main.cpp. The solution is to have the function declaration in `cpp_sources` and the implementation in `cuda_sources`, OR to have a forward declaration in `cpp_sources`. Let me separate: have the host function prototype in `cpp_sources` and the kernel + host function definition in `cuda_sources`.

this doesn't work well with vectors\n # Let me use a direct load instead for simplicity\n w_ptr = w_base_ptr + w_idx\n w_val = tl.load(w_ptr, mask=valid, other=0.0)\n else:\n w_ptr = w_base_ptr + w_idx\n w_val = tl.load(w_ptr, mask=valid, other=0.0)\n \n sum_val += in_val * w_val\n \n # Store conv output for later normalization\n out_idx_ptr = output_ptr + ((n * C_out + group_start + c_local) * T_out + t)\n tl.store(out_idx_ptr, sum_val, mask=valid)\n \n # Now compute mean and variance\n # We need to do this sequentially or with reductions\n # First pass: compute sum\n total_sum = 0.0\n for start_idx in range(0, total_outputs, BLOCK_SIZE):\n idx = start_idx + tid\n valid = idx < total_outputs\n \n c_local = idx // T_out\n t = idx % T_out\n \n val_ptr = output_ptr + ((n * C_out + group_start + c_local) * T_out + t)\n val = tl.load(val_ptr, mask=valid, other=0.0)\n total_sum += tl.sum(val)\n \n mean = total_sum / total_outputs\n \n # Second pass: compute variance\n total_sq = 0.0\n for start_idx in range(0, total_outputs, BLOCK_SIZE):\n idx = start_idx + tid\n valid = idx < total_outputs\n \n c_local = idx // T_out\n t = idx % T_out\n \n val_ptr = output_ptr + ((n * C_out + group_start + c_local) * T_out + t)\n val = tl.load(val_ptr, mask=valid, other=0.0)\n diff = val - With the given sizes: - N=16, C_in=128, C_out=256, K=3 - c_per_group = 256/32 = 8 - total_outputs = 8 * 1024 = 8192 - weight_size = 8 * 128 * 3 = 3072 - shmem = 2 + 8192 + 3072 + 32 * (32 is the number of warps for 256 threads? On H100, max shared memory per block is 227KB, so this fits. But 8 warps * 4 = 32 bytes for warp_sums. And 8 * 8192 = 65536 threads worth of output? No wait, it's just 8192 floats. So 45KB is fine. But wait, the problem is that each block processes one (n, g) pair. With N=16 and num_groups=32, there are 16 * 32 = 512 blocks. Each block uses 256 threads. This should saturate the GPU well. However, each warp computing 8192 elements with 256 threads = each thread does 8192/256 = 32 operations. And each operation is a multiply-accumulate over C_in*K = 384 operations. So the total FLOPs per thread in the conv part is 32 * 384 = 12288 FMAs. That's actually quite a lot of compute per thread, which is good. Actually wait, the conv is c_per_group * T_out * C_in * K = 8 * 1024 * 128 * 3 = 3,145,728 multiplications per (n, g) block. And there are 256 threads = 12288 multiplications per thread. At ~67 TFLOPS for FP32, this is very achievable. But looking at the memory access pattern: each thread reads from input across all C_in and K positions. This is very inefficient because threads within a warp will access very different locations in memory, causing many memory transactions. This is because t varies across threads. To improve, I should think about different parallelism. Let me reconsider the approach. Actually, for a 1D conv with stride=1, padding=1, dilation=1, kernel=3, and T_in=T_out=1024: - Each output position t depends on input positions t-1, t, t+1 - For a given output channel c_out and input channel c_in, position t reads from input at c_in, t-1, t, t+1 The most efficient way is to: 1. Have each block process a tile of time positions and a group of output channels 2. Load input for the required time extent into shared memory 3. Then compute the convolution in a more coalesced way But for simplicity, my current approach might actually be okay. The shared memory for weights is good because weights are reused across all threads and time positions. Input reads are going to be strided but sequential across threads in a warp (since t varies by 1 per thread when reading a specific position). Wait, let's trace the memory access: - Thread 0 processes t=0, thread 1 processes t=1, etc. - When they read input position `t_base + k`, they read consecutive memory locations - So for a fixed c_in and k, all threads in a warp read consecutive float elements - This is actually COALESCED! Great! For each c_in and k iteration, the memory access is perfectly coalesced across the warp. And weights are in shared memory, so no global memory access for weights after loading. This is actually a pretty good access pattern. The algorithm should work fairly well. Let me try to compile it again with the library fix. I'll also consider reducing the code complexity if needed. Wait, actually I could compute local statistics in registers, reduce to global mean/var, and then normalize and write back. That avoids needing extra shared memory for the entire group's data, just for reduction. Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void groupnorm_kernel(float* data, int N, int C, int T, int num_groups, float eps, float* gamma, float* beta, bool affine) { int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int c_per_group = C / num_groups; int group_start = g * c_per_group; int group_elements = c_per_group * T; // Each thread processes a subset of elements float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < group_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = data[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t]; local_sum += val; local_sq += val * val; } // Warp reduce local_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq); // Block reduce via shared memory __shared__ float s_mean[1]; __shared__ float s_var[1]; __shared__ float warp_sums[8]; __shared__ float warp_sq[8]; int lane = threadIdx.x & 31; int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; if (lane == 0 && warp_id < 8) { warp_sums[warp_id] = local_sum; warp_sq[warp_id] = local_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float block_sum = (lane < 8) ? warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; float block_sq = (lane < 8) ? warp_sq[lane] : 0.0f; block_sum = warp_reduce_sum(block_sum); block_sq = warp_reduce_sum(block_sq); if (lane == 0) { float mean = block_sum / group_elements; float mean_sq = block_sq / group_elements; s_mean[0] = mean; s_var[0] = mean_sq - mean * mean; // population variance } } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); // Normalize in-place for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < group_elements; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = data[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t]; val = (val - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { val = val * gamma[c_out] + beta[c_out]; } data[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t] = val; } } ``` Wait, using mean_sq - mean*mean for variance is mathematically equivalent but numerically less stable. PyTorch might use E[x^2] - E[x]^2 or the two-pass approach. Let me check which is more likely to match PyTorch. Actually, PyTorch GroupNorm uses Welford's algorithm (which is numerically stable) or a two-pass approach in its CUDA kernel. Let me look at how PyTorch computes variance in group norm... Regardless, the difference between these methods for values of O(1) should be well below 1e-4. So either should work. But to be safe, let me use the same approach as PyTorch's two-pass algorithm: compute mean first, then compute variance as E[(x - mean)^2]. I can do the variance computation in the same kernel: 1. First loop: compute local_sum 2. Reduce to global mean 3. Second loop: compute local_sq_diff = sum of (x - mean)^2 4. Reduce to global var 5. Third loop: normalize This is 3 loops over the data plus reduction steps. For group_elements = 8192 and block_size = 256, each thread processes 32 elements per loop. So 3 * 32 = 96 loads per thread. On H100, this is very fast if memory bandwidth is sufficient. But wait, the conv output is in GPU global memory. Reading it 3 times (plus 1 write) = 4 * 8192 * 4 bytes per block = 131 KB per block. With 512 blocks total (16*32), total memory traffic = 512 * 131 KB = ~67 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, this is ~20 microseconds for memory bandwidth alone. That's quite fast. Let me write this kernel and test it. Actually, I can combine pass 2 and 3 into a single pass by having each thread keep a running sum and running sum of squares, then compute mean and variance from those. But that's the same as E[x^2] - E[x]^2 approach. Using E[x^2] - E[x]^2 requires only 2 passes total (accumulate sum and sum_sq, reduce, then normalize). This saves one pass over the data. I'll use this approach but be careful: var = E[x^2] - E[x]^2. For the sizes here, this should be numerically stable enough (8192 elements, values ~O(1)). Now let me write the code and compile. PyTorch's group norm computes: ```python mean = x.mean(dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True) # mean over channels in group and time var = x.var(dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True, unbiased=False) # population variance ``` In CUDA, this is implemented with block-level or warp-level reductions. The variance is computed as mean of squared deviations or using Welford. I'll use the two-pass approach: 1. Compute mean 2. Let me recalculate: with my weight init bound = 1/sqrt(384), and random uniform weights, the expected conv output variance is: E[w^2] = a^2/3 = 1/(3*384) = 1/1152 per product. For 384 products: variance = 384/1152 = 1/3 ≈ 0.333. Std = sqrt(0.333) ≈ 0.577. If TF32 reduces each operand to 10 bits, the weights and inputs are both rounded. The input is from a normal distribution. Rounding to 10 bits adds noise with variance ~2^-20. Very small. But for weights, rounding to 10 bits means the weight values themselves change. If weights are in [-0.05, 0.05], the rounding step is ~2^-10 * 0.05 ≈ 5e-5. This changes the actual weight values used in computation. Since weights are fixed during inference, each output element depends on the specific rounded weights. The difference from true FP32 weights can be significant. BUT the checker should be using the same weights for reference and ModelNew. The reference's conv uses the stored weights (which are FP32 in memory). The TF32 computation rounds them inside the tensor core. My kernel reads the same FP32 memory values but doesn't round them before multiply. So yes, this IS the source of difference. s_warp_sum[lane] : 0.0f; float block_sq = (lane < 8) ? s_warp_sq[lane] : 0.0f; block_sum = warp_reduce_sum(block_sum); block_sq = warp_reduce_sum(block_sq); if (lane == 0) { float mean = block_sum / float(total); float mean_sq = block_sq / float(total); s_mean[0] = mean; s_var[0] = mean_sq - mean * mean; } } __syncthreads(); float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); // Pass 2: normalize in-place for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = data[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t]; val = (val - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { val = val * gamma[c_out] + beta[c_out]; } data[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t] = val; } } torch::Tensor group_norm_cuda( torch::Tensor input, int64_t num_groups, double eps, bool affine, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta ) { int N = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int T = input.size(2); // In-place operation float* data = input.data_ptr<float>(); dim3 blocks(N, num_groups); int threads = 256; group_norm_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( data, N, C, T, num_groups, static_cast<float>(eps), affine, affine ? gamma.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, affine ? beta.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr ); return input; } ''' group_norm_ext = load_inline( name="group_norm_v1", cpp_sources=[cpp_source], cuda_sources=[cuda_source], functions=["group_norm_cuda"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], extra_ldflags=["-static-libstdc++"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, num_groups, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1, dilation=1, eps=1e-5, affine=True): super().__init__() self.conv = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=False) self.num_groups = num_groups self.eps = eps if affine: self.gamma = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(out_channels)) self.beta = nn.Parameter(torch.zeros(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('gamma', None) self.register_parameter('beta', None) def forward(self, x): out = self.conv(x) return group_norm_ext.group_norm_cuda(out, self.num_groups, self.eps, self.gamma is not None, self.gamma, self.beta) ``` Wait, if I use `self.conv = nn.Conv1d(...)`, the checker copies `conv.weight` from reference to ModelNew if names match. The `a=math.sqrt(5)` corresponds to `nonlinearity='leaky_relu'` with negative_slope=sqrt(5). As I computed earlier, the effective bound is `1/sqrt(fan_in)`. BUT: is the `reset_parameters` in PyTorch exactly this? Let me verify by looking at PyTorch source: ```python # torch/nn/modules/conv.py def reset_parameters(self): init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) ``` And in `torch/nn/init.py`: ```python def kaiming_uniform_(tensor, a=0, mode='fan_in', nonlinearity='leaky_relu'): fan = _calculate_correct_fan(tensor, mode) gain = calculate_gain(nonlinearity, a) std = gain / math.sqrt(fan) bound = math.sqrt(3.0) * std with torch.no_grad(): return tensor.uniform_(-bound, bound) ``` With `a=math.sqrt(5)` and `nonlinearity='leaky_relu'`: ```python def calculate_gain(nonlinearity, param=None): ... elif nonlinearity == 'leaky_relu': negative_slope = param if param else 0.01 return math.sqrt(2.0 / (1 + negative_slope ** 2)) ``` So: ``` gain = sqrt(2 / (1 + 5)) = sqrt(1/3) std = sqrt(1/3) / sqrt(fan_in) bound = sqrt(3) * sqrt(1/3) / sqrt(fan_in) = 1 / sqrt(fan_in) ``` This confirms my earlier calculation. So `nn.Conv1d` initializes weights with `uniform_(-1/sqrt(fan_in), 1/sqrt(fan_in))`. And I know my manual init also uses `bound = math.sqrt(1.0 / fan_in)`, which is exactly `1/sqrt(fan_in)`. So both match. Great, let me write the final code. Now, the custom GN kernel. I'll keep it simple and use the mean_sq - mean^2 approach. But for the mean/var computation, I access shared memory instead of global memory, which is much faster and avoids redundant global memory traffic. Without shared memory caching, in my current plan, I do: - Pass 1: 1 read per element (accumulate sum and sq) - Pass 2: 1 read + 1 write per element (normalize) Total: 2 reads + 1 write. With shared memory caching: - Load: 1 read per element (global to shared) - Compute: 0 global reads/writes (shared memory only) - Store: 1 write per element (shared to global) Total: 1 read + 1 write. This saves 1 read pass! For 32MB of data, saving 16MB of reads is ~5 microseconds at 3.35 TB/s. This is actually meaningful for such a fast operation. Let me implement shared memory caching. I'll load the entire group's data into shared memory first. Wait, but with c_per_group=8 and T=1024, total elements = 8192 floats = 32768 bytes. With 256 threads, each thread loads 32 floats = 128 bytes. The shared memory layout mirrors the global memory layout. Let me write this: ```cuda __global__ void group_norm_kernel(...) { int n = blockIdx.x; int g = blockIdx.y; int c_per_group = C / num_groups; int group_start = g * c_per_group; int total = c_per_group * T; extern __shared__ float s_data[]; // s_data size = total floats // Load data into shared memory int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c_out = group_start + c_local; s_data[idx] = input[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute mean and variance from shared memory float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = s_data[idx]; local_sum += val; local_sq += val * val; } // ... reduction ... __syncthreads(); // Normalize and write back float mean = s_mean[0]; float inv_std = rsqrtf(s_var[0] + eps); for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { int c_local = idx / T; int t = idx % T; int c_out = group_start + c_local; float val = (s_data[idx] - mean) * inv_std; if (affine) { val = val * gamma[c_out] + beta[c_out]; } output[((n * C) + c_out) * T + t] = val; } } ``` Shared memory size per block: 8192 floats = 32768 bytes = 32 KB. Plus a few bytes for reduction. This is well within limits. With shared memory caching, the global memory traffic is: - Read: total * N * num_groups * sizeof(float) = 16 * 256 * 1024 * 4 = 16MB - Write: 16MB Total: 32MB Without shared memory caching (two passes reading from global): - Read: 2 * 16MB = 32MB - Write: 16MB Total: 48MB Shared memory saves 33% memory bandwidth! This should make the kernel faster. Let me implement this. Actually, for the shared memory approach, there might be bank conflicts. The access pattern is: - Thread 0 reads s_data[0], s_data[256], s_data[512], ... - Thread 1 reads s_data[1], s_data[257], s_data[513], ... On H100 (and Ampere), shared memory has 32 banks. Consecutive addresses map to consecutive banks. So thread 0 accesses bank 0, thread 1 accesses bank 1, etc. This is conflict-free! Because each thread in a warp accesses a different bank. Wait, in the first phase (loading from global to shared): - Thread 0 writes s_data[0] - Thread 1 writes s_data[1] - etc. This is also conflict-free. Actually, I'll try to make the kernel slightly more efficient by using single-pass loading + reduction, storing elements in shared memory, and then normalizing in a second pass. Wait, I said shared memory caching saves a read pass. With shared memory: 1. Load all elements to shared memory (1 global read) 2. Compute sum and sum_sq from shared memory (shared reads) 3. Reduce to mean and var 4. Normalize and write from shared memory (1 global write) Total: 1 global read, 1 global write. Without shared memory (two-pass): 1. Shared memory saves 1 read pass. Hmm, I thought the GN kernel with shared memory saves memory within the kernel itself, but the conv still writes to global memory first. So the total data movement is the same. Without shared memory, it would access global memory twice for read and once for write. GN: reads output, writes final_output With my shared-memory GN: 1. Conv: reads input + weights, writes conv_output 2. GN: reads conv_output once into shared mem, writes final_output Total memory reads: input + weights + conv_output Total memory writes: conv_output + final_output If PyTorch GN also reads conv_output once and writes final_output, then total memory is the same. So the shared memory optimization doesn't reduce overall data movement; it just makes the GN kernel itself more efficient by avoiding redundant reads of conv_output. PyTorch GroupNorm computes variance as `mean((x - mean)^2)`. My kernel computes: 1. `mean = sum(x) / N` 2. `sum_sq = sum(x^2)` 3. `var = sum_sq / N - mean^2` This is `E[x^2] - E[x]^2`, not `E[(x - mean)^2]`! Wait, `E[(x - mean)^2] = E[x^2 - 2*x*mean + mean^2] = E[x^2] - 2*mean*E[x] + mean^2 = E[x^2] - 2*mean^2 + mean^2 = E[x^2] - mean^2`. So `E[x^2] - E[x]^2` is indeed equal to `E[(x - mean)^2]`. for (int idx = tid; idx < total; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = s_data[idx]; local_sum += val; local_sq += val * val; } // Warp reduction local_sum = warp_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sq = warp_reduce_sum(local_sq);
